	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Bundesliga</span></h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		<div id="contentSub2"></div>
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Association football league in Germany</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the German men's football league. For the German women's football league, see <a href="/wiki/Frauen-Bundesliga" title="Frauen-Bundesliga">Frauen-Bundesliga</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Bundesliga_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Bundesliga (disambiguation)">Bundesliga (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>

<p>The <b>Bundesliga</b> (<small>German:&#32;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈbʊndəsˌliːɡa]</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Bundesliga.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/03/De-Bundesliga.ogg" class="internal" title="De-Bundesliga.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; <a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#8201;</span>'Federal League&#39;), sometimes referred to as the <b>Fußball-Bundesliga</b> (<small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˌfuːsbal-]</a></span>) or <b>1. Bundesliga</b> (<small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˌeːɐ̯stə-]</a></span>), is a professional <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">association football</a> league in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>. At the top of the <a href="/wiki/German_football_league_system" title="German football league system">German football league system</a>, the Bundesliga is Germany's primary football competition. The Bundesliga comprises 18 teams and operates on a system of <a href="/wiki/Promotion_and_relegation" title="Promotion and relegation">promotion and relegation</a> with the <a href="/wiki/2._Bundesliga" title="2. Bundesliga">2. Bundesliga</a>. Seasons run from August to May. Most games are played on Saturdays and Sundays, with a few games played on weekdays. All of the Bundesliga <a href="/wiki/Football_club_(association_football)" title="Football club (association football)">clubs</a> qualify for the <a href="/wiki/DFB-Pokal" title="DFB-Pokal">DFB-Pokal</a>. The winner of the Bundesliga qualifies for the <a href="/wiki/DFL-Supercup" title="DFL-Supercup">DFL-Supercup</a>.
</p><p>Fifty-six clubs have competed in the Bundesliga since its founding. <a href="/wiki/FC_Bayern_Munich" title="FC Bayern Munich">Bayern Munich</a> has won the title 31 times, the most among Bundesliga clubs. However, the Bundesliga has seen other champions, with <a href="/wiki/Borussia_Dortmund" title="Borussia Dortmund">Borussia Dortmund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamburger_SV" title="Hamburger SV">Hamburger SV</a>, <a href="/wiki/SV_Werder_Bremen" title="SV Werder Bremen">Werder Bremen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borussia_M%C3%B6nchengladbach" title="Borussia Mönchengladbach">Borussia Mönchengladbach</a>, and <a href="/wiki/VfB_Stuttgart" title="VfB Stuttgart">VfB Stuttgart</a> most prominent among them. The Bundesliga is one of the top national leagues, ranked fourth in Europe according to <a href="/wiki/UEFA" title="UEFA">UEFA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/UEFA_coefficient#Men&#39;s_Association_coefficient" title="UEFA coefficient">league coefficient</a> ranking for the 2021–22 season, based on performances in European competitions over the past five seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The Bundesliga led the UEFA ranking <a href="/wiki/UEFA_coefficient#Men&#39;s_Association_coefficient" title="UEFA coefficient">from 1976 to 1984 and in 1990</a>. It has also produced the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_coefficient#Top_club_by_period" title="UEFA coefficient">continent's top-rated club</a> seven times. Bundesliga clubs have won eight <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">UEFA Champions League</a>, seven <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Europa League</a>, four <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Cup_Winners%27_Cup" title="UEFA Cup Winners&#39; Cup">European Cup Winners' Cup</a>, two <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Super_Cup" title="UEFA Super Cup">UEFA Super Cup</a>, two <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Club_World_Cup" title="FIFA Club World Cup">FIFA Club World Cup</a>, and three <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_Cup_(football)" title="Intercontinental Cup (football)">Intercontinental Cup</a> titles. Its players have accumulated nine <a href="/wiki/Ballon_d%27Or" title="Ballon d&#39;Or">Ballon d'Or</a> awards, two <a href="/wiki/The_Best_FIFA_Men%27s_Player" title="The Best FIFA Men&#39;s Player">The Best FIFA Men's Player</a> awards, four <a href="/wiki/European_Golden_Shoe" title="European Golden Shoe">European Golden Shoe</a>, and three <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Men%27s_Player_of_the_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA Men&#39;s Player of the Year">UEFA Men's Player of the Year</a> awards including <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Club_Footballer_of_the_Year" title="UEFA Club Footballer of the Year">UEFA Club Footballer of the Year</a>.
</p><p>The Bundesliga is the number one football league in the world in terms of average attendance; out of all sports, its average of 45,134 fans per game during the 2011–12 season was the second-highest of any sports league in the world after the American <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sportbusiness1_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sportbusiness1-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The Bundesliga is broadcast on television in over 200 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Bundesliga was founded in 1962 in <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and the first season started in 1963–64. The structure and organisation of the Bundesliga, along with Germany's other football leagues, have undergone frequent changes. The Bundesliga was founded by the <a href="/wiki/German_Football_Association" title="German Football Association">Deutscher Fußball-Bund</a> (English: German Football Association), but is now operated by the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Fu%C3%9Fball_Liga" title="Deutsche Fußball Liga">Deutsche Fußball Liga</a> (English: German Football League).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Bundesliga is composed of two divisions: the 1. Bundesliga (although it is rarely referred to with the <i>First</i> prefix), and, below that, the 2. Bundesliga (2nd Bundesliga), which has been the second tier of German football <a href="/wiki/Introduction_of_the_2._Bundesliga" title="Introduction of the 2. Bundesliga">since 1974</a>. The Bundesligen (plural) are professional leagues. Since 2008, the <a href="/wiki/3._Liga" title="3. Liga">3. Liga</a> (3rd League) in Germany has also been a professional league, but may not be called Bundesliga because the league is run by the German Football Association (DFB) and not, as are the two Bundesligen, by the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Fu%C3%9Fball_Liga" title="Deutsche Fußball Liga">German Football League</a> (DFL).
</p><p>Below the level of the 3. Liga, leagues are generally subdivided on a regional basis. For example, the Regionalligen are currently made up of Nord (North), Nordost (Northeast), Süd (South), Südwest (Southwest) and West divisions. Below this are thirteen parallel divisions, most of which are called Oberligen (upper leagues) which represent federal states or large urban and geographical areas. The levels below the <a href="/wiki/Oberliga_(football)" title="Oberliga (football)">Oberligen</a> differ between the local areas. The league structure has changed frequently and typically reflects the degree of participation in the sport in various parts of the country. In the early 1990s, changes were driven by the <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">reunification of Germany</a> and the subsequent integration of the national league of East Germany.
</p><p>Every team in the two Bundesligen must have a licence to play in the league, or else they are relegated into the regional leagues. To obtain a licence, teams must be financially healthy and meet certain standards of conduct as organisations.
</p><p>As in other national leagues, there are significant benefits to being in the top division:
</p>
<ul><li>A greater share of television broadcast licence revenues goes to 1. Bundesliga sides.</li>
<li>1. Bundesliga teams draw significantly greater levels of fan support. Average attendance in the first league is 42,673 per game—more than twice the average of the 2. Bundesliga.</li>
<li>Greater exposure through television and higher attendance levels helps 1. Bundesliga teams attract the most lucrative sponsorships.</li>
<li>1. Bundesliga teams develop substantial financial muscle through the combination of television and gate revenues, sponsorships and marketing of their team brands. This allows them to attract and retain skilled players from domestic and international sources and to construct first-class stadium facilities.</li></ul>
<p>The 1. Bundesliga is financially strong, and the 2. Bundesliga has begun to evolve in a similar direction, becoming more stable organizationally and financially, and reflecting an increasingly higher standard of professional play.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arena_Auf_Schalke_hosting_Schalke_04_vs_Dortmund_in_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Arena_Auf_Schalke_hosting_Schalke_04_vs_Dortmund_in_2009.jpg/230px-Arena_Auf_Schalke_hosting_Schalke_04_vs_Dortmund_in_2009.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Arena_Auf_Schalke_hosting_Schalke_04_vs_Dortmund_in_2009.jpg/345px-Arena_Auf_Schalke_hosting_Schalke_04_vs_Dortmund_in_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Arena_Auf_Schalke_hosting_Schalke_04_vs_Dortmund_in_2009.jpg/460px-Arena_Auf_Schalke_hosting_Schalke_04_vs_Dortmund_in_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arena_Auf_Schalke_hosting_Schalke_04_vs_Dortmund_in_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Borussia_Dortmund" title="Borussia Dortmund">Borussia Dortmund</a> against rivals <a href="/wiki/FC_Schalke_04" title="FC Schalke 04">Schalke</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Revierderby" title="Revierderby">Revierderby</a>, in the Bundesliga in 2009</div></div></div>
<p>Internationally, the most well-known German clubs include <a href="/wiki/FC_Bayern_Munich" title="FC Bayern Munich">Bayern Munich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borussia_Dortmund" title="Borussia Dortmund">Borussia Dortmund</a>, <a href="/wiki/FC_Schalke_04" title="FC Schalke 04">Schalke 04</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamburger_SV" title="Hamburger SV">Hamburger SV</a>, <a href="/wiki/VfB_Stuttgart" title="VfB Stuttgart">VfB Stuttgart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borussia_M%C3%B6nchengladbach" title="Borussia Mönchengladbach">Borussia Mönchengladbach</a>, <a href="/wiki/SV_Werder_Bremen" title="SV Werder Bremen">Werder Bremen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bayer_Leverkusen" class="mw-redirect" title="Bayer Leverkusen">Bayer Leverkusen</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Hamburger SV was the only club to have played continuously in the Bundesliga since its foundation, until 12 May 2018, when the club was relegated for the first time.
</p><p>In the 2008–09 season, the Bundesliga reinstated an earlier German system of promotion and relegation, which had been in use from 1981 until 1991:
</p>
<ul><li>The bottom two finishers in the Bundesliga are automatically relegated to the 2. Bundesliga, with the top two finishers in the 2. Bundesliga taking their places.</li>
<li>The third-from-bottom club in the Bundesliga will play a two-legged tie with the third-place team from the 2. Bundesliga, with the winner taking up the final place in the following season's Bundesliga.</li></ul>
<p>From 1992 until 2008, a different system was used, in which the bottom three finishers of the Bundesliga had been automatically relegated, to be replaced by the top three finishers in the 2. Bundesliga. From 1963 until 1981 two, or later three, teams had been relegated from the Bundesliga automatically, while promotion had been decided either completely or partially in <a href="/wiki/Promotion_to_the_Bundesliga" title="Promotion to the Bundesliga">promotion play-offs</a>.
</p><p>The season starts in early August<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and lasts until late May, with a winter break of six weeks (mid-December through to the end of January). Starting with the 2021–22 season, kick off times have been changed with Friday matches starting at 8:30&#160;pm, Saturdays at 3:30&#160;pm and 6:30&#160;pm, and Sundays at 3:30&#160;pm, 5:30&#160;pm and 7:30&#160;pm.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_German_football" title="History of German football">History of German football</a></div>
<p>Prior to the formation of the Bundesliga, German football was played at an amateur level in a large number of sub-regional leagues until, in 1949, part-time (semi-) professionalism was introduced and only five regional <i>Oberligen (Premier Leagues)</i> remained. Regional champions and runners-up played a series of playoff matches for the right to compete in a final game for the national championship. On 28 January 1900, a national association, the <a href="/wiki/German_Football_Association" title="German Football Association">Deutscher Fußball Bund</a> (DFB) had been founded in <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a> with 86 member clubs. The first recognised national championship team was <a href="/wiki/VfB_Leipzig" class="mw-redirect" title="VfB Leipzig">VfB Leipzig</a>, who beat <a href="/wiki/DFC_Prague" class="mw-redirect" title="DFC Prague">DFC Prague</a> 7–2 in a game played at <a href="/wiki/Altona,_Hamburg" title="Altona, Hamburg">Altona</a> on 31 May 1903.
</p><p>Through the 1950s, there were continued calls for the formation of a central professional league, especially as professional leagues in other countries began to draw Germany's best players away from the semi-professional domestic leagues. At the international level, the German game began to falter as German teams often fared poorly against professional teams from other countries. A key supporter of the central league concept was national team head coach Sepp Herberger who said, "If we want to remain competitive internationally, we have to raise our expectations at the national level."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Inline_citation#When_you_must_use_inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Inline citation"><span title="the statement is simple enough, but should be referenced. (September 2020)">quote citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, in East Germany, a separate league was established with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/DDR-Oberliga" title="DDR-Oberliga">DS-Oberliga</a> (Deutscher Sportausschuss Oberliga) in 1950. The league was renamed the Football Oberliga DFV in 1958 and was generally referred to simply as the DDR-Liga or DDR-Oberliga. The league fielded 14 teams with two relegation spots.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation">Foundation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Foundation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/File:Auswahl_der_Bundesligavereine_1963-64.png" class="image"><img alt="Auswahl der Bundesligavereine 1963-64.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Auswahl_der_Bundesligavereine_1963-64.png/400px-Auswahl_der_Bundesligavereine_1963-64.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="458" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Auswahl_der_Bundesligavereine_1963-64.png/600px-Auswahl_der_Bundesligavereine_1963-64.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Auswahl_der_Bundesligavereine_1963-64.png/800px-Auswahl_der_Bundesligavereine_1963-64.png 2x" data-file-width="1310" data-file-height="1500" /></a> 
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Introduction_of_the_Bundesliga" title="Introduction of the Bundesliga">Introduction of the Bundesliga</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westfalenhalle_1_Dortmund.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Westfalenhalle_1_Dortmund.JPG/220px-Westfalenhalle_1_Dortmund.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Westfalenhalle_1_Dortmund.JPG/330px-Westfalenhalle_1_Dortmund.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Westfalenhalle_1_Dortmund.JPG/440px-Westfalenhalle_1_Dortmund.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3644" data-file-height="1860" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westfalenhalle_1_Dortmund.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Bundesliga was founded at the annual DFB convention at the <a href="/wiki/Westfalenhallen" title="Westfalenhallen">Westfalenhallen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a> on 28 July 1962</div></div></div>
<p>The defeat of the national team by Yugoslavia (0–1) in a 1962 World Cup quarter-final game in Chile was one impetus (of many) towards the formation of a national league. At the annual <a href="/wiki/Deutscher_Fu%C3%9Fball-Bund" class="mw-redirect" title="Deutscher Fußball-Bund">DFB</a> convention under new DFB president <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6smann" title="Hermann Gösmann">Hermann Gösmann</a> (elected that very day) the Bundesliga was created in <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Westfalenhallen" title="Westfalenhallen">Westfalenhallen</a> on 28 July 1962 to begin play starting with the <a href="/wiki/1963%E2%80%9364_Bundesliga" title="1963–64 Bundesliga">1963–64 season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the time, there were five <i>Oberligen</i> (premier leagues) in place representing West Germany's North, South, West, Southwest, and Berlin. East Germany, behind the Iron Curtain, maintained its separate league structure. 46 clubs applied for admission to the new league. 16 teams were selected based on their success on the field, economic criteria and representation of the various Oberligen.
</p>
<ul><li>From <a href="/wiki/Oberliga_Nord_(1947%E2%80%9363)" class="mw-redirect" title="Oberliga Nord (1947–63)">Oberliga Nord</a>: <a href="/wiki/Eintracht_Braunschweig" title="Eintracht Braunschweig">Eintracht Braunschweig</a>, <a href="/wiki/SV_Werder_Bremen" title="SV Werder Bremen">Werder Bremen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamburger_SV" title="Hamburger SV">Hamburger SV</a></li>
<li>From <a href="/wiki/Oberliga_West_(1947%E2%80%9363)" class="mw-redirect" title="Oberliga West (1947–63)">Oberliga West</a>: <a href="/wiki/Borussia_Dortmund" title="Borussia Dortmund">Borussia Dortmund</a>, <a href="/wiki/1._FC_K%C3%B6ln" title="1. FC Köln">1. FC Köln</a>, <a href="/wiki/MSV_Duisburg" title="MSV Duisburg">Meidericher SV</a> (now <a href="/wiki/MSV_Duisburg" title="MSV Duisburg">MSV Duisburg</a>), <a href="/wiki/SC_Preu%C3%9Fen_M%C3%BCnster" title="SC Preußen Münster">Preußen Münster</a>, <a href="/wiki/FC_Schalke_04" title="FC Schalke 04">Schalke 04</a></li>
<li>From <a href="/wiki/Oberliga_S%C3%BCdwest_(1945%E2%80%9363)" class="mw-redirect" title="Oberliga Südwest (1945–63)">Oberliga Südwest</a>: <a href="/wiki/1._FC_Kaiserslautern" title="1. FC Kaiserslautern">1. FC Kaiserslautern</a>, <a href="/wiki/1._FC_Saarbr%C3%BCcken" title="1. FC Saarbrücken">1. FC Saarbrücken</a></li>
<li>From <a href="/wiki/Oberliga_S%C3%BCd_(1945%E2%80%9363)" class="mw-redirect" title="Oberliga Süd (1945–63)">Oberliga Süd</a>: <a href="/wiki/Eintracht_Frankfurt" title="Eintracht Frankfurt">Eintracht Frankfurt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karlsruher_SC" title="Karlsruher SC">Karlsruher SC</a>, <a href="/wiki/1._FC_N%C3%BCrnberg" title="1. FC Nürnberg">1. FC Nürnberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/TSV_1860_Munich" title="TSV 1860 Munich">1860 Munich</a>, <a href="/wiki/VfB_Stuttgart" title="VfB Stuttgart">VfB Stuttgart</a></li>
<li>From <a href="/wiki/Oberliga_Berlin_(1945%E2%80%9363)" class="mw-redirect" title="Oberliga Berlin (1945–63)">Oberliga Berlin</a>: <a href="/wiki/Hertha_BSC" title="Hertha BSC">Hertha BSC</a></li></ul>
<p>The first Bundesliga games were played on 24 August 1963. Early favourite 1. FC Köln was the first Bundesliga champion (with 45:15 points) over second place clubs Meidericher SV and Eintracht Frankfurt (both 39:21).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reunification">Reunification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reunification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a>, the East German leagues were merged into the West German system. <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_Dresden" title="Dynamo Dresden">Dynamo Dresden</a> and <a href="/wiki/FC_Hansa_Rostock" title="FC Hansa Rostock">FC Hansa Rostock</a> were seeded into the top-tier Bundesliga division, with other clubs being sorted into lower tiers.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition_format">Competition format</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Competition format">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elia_son.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Elia_son.JPG/200px-Elia_son.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Elia_son.JPG/300px-Elia_son.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Elia_son.JPG/400px-Elia_son.JPG 2x" data-file-width="439" data-file-height="534" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elia_son.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Son_Heung-min" title="Son Heung-min">Son Heung-min</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hamburger_SV" title="Hamburger SV">Hamburger SV</a> against <a href="/wiki/Eljero_Elia" title="Eljero Elia">Eljero Elia</a> of <a href="/wiki/SV_Werder_Bremen" title="SV Werder Bremen">Werder Bremen</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nordderby" title="Nordderby">Nordderby</a></div></div></div>
<p>The German football champion is decided strictly by play in the Bundesliga. Each club plays every other club once at home and once away. Originally, a victory was worth two points, with one point for a draw and none for a loss. Since the 1995–96 season, a victory has been worth three points, while a draw remains worth a single point, and zero points are given for a loss. The club with the most points at the end of the season becomes German champion. Currently, the top four clubs in the table qualify automatically for the group phase of the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">UEFA Champions League</a>. The two teams at the bottom of the table are relegated into the 2. Bundesliga, while the top two teams in the 2. Bundesliga are promoted. The 16th-placed team (third-last), and the third-placed team in the 2. Bundesliga play a two-leg play-off match. The winner of this match plays the next season in the Bundesliga, and the loser in the 2. Bundesliga.
</p><p>If teams are level on points, tie-breakers are applied in the following order:
</p>
<ol><li>Goal difference for the entire season</li>
<li>Total goals scored for the entire season</li>
<li>Head-to-head results (total points)</li>
<li>Head-to-head goals scored</li>
<li>Head-to-head away goals scored</li>
<li>Total away goals scored for the entire season</li></ol>
<p>If two clubs are still tied after all of these tie-breakers have been applied, a single match is held at a neutral site to determine the placement. However, this has never been necessary in the history of the Bundesliga.
</p><p>In terms of team selection, matchday squads must have no more than five non-EU representatives. Nine substitutes are permitted to be selected, from which three can be used in the duration of the game.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_league_structure">Changes in league structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Changes in league structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Number of teams:
<ul><li>1963–64 to 1964–65: 16</li>
<li>1965–66 to 1990–91: 18</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/1991%E2%80%9392_Bundesliga" title="1991–92 Bundesliga">1991–92</a>: 20, while the <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East German</a> league was being included after <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a></li>
<li>Since 1992–93: 18</li></ul></li>
<li>Number of teams relegated (automatic relegation except as noted):
<ul><li>1963–64 to 1973–74: 2</li>
<li>1974–75 to 1980–81: 3</li>
<li>1981–82 to 1990–91: 2 automatic plus the 16th-place team in the First Bundesliga played a two-leg relegation match against the third-place team of the Second Bundesliga for the final spot in the First Bundesliga</li>
<li>1991–92: 4</li>
<li>1992–93 to 2007–08: 3</li>
<li>Since 2008–09: 2 automatic plus the 16th-place team in the First Bundesliga playing a two-leg relegation match against the third-place team of the Second Bundesliga for the final spot in the First Bundesliga</li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Qualification_for_European_competitions">Qualification for European competitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Qualification for European competitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1st, 2nd, 3rd, and 4th place: Group stage of <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">UEFA Champions League</a></li>
<li>5th place: Group stage of <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Europa League</a></li>
<li>6th place: Third qualifying round of Europa League</li>
<li>Until the 2016–17 season, an additional place in the Europa League could also be granted via the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Fair_Play_ranking" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA Fair Play ranking">UEFA Fair Play</a> mechanism. This rule was maintained from the UEFA Cup. The last Bundesliga team to gain entry to the UEFA Cup via the fair play rule was <a href="/wiki/FSV_Mainz_05" class="mw-redirect" title="FSV Mainz 05">Mainz 05</a> in <a href="/wiki/2005%E2%80%9306_UEFA_Cup" title="2005–06 UEFA Cup">2005–06</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/DFB-Pokal" title="DFB-Pokal">DFB-Pokal</a> (German Cup) winner: Qualifies for group stage of Europa League regardless of league position.
<ul><li>Until 2015–16, if the Cup winner qualified for the Champions League, the cup winner's place in the Europa League went to the defeated cup finalist if it had not already qualified for European competition, although the defeated cup finalist would enter the competition a stage earlier than if it had won the Cup. This rule was retained from the Europa League's predecessor, the UEFA Cup. From 2015 to 2016, the runners-up no longer qualify for the Europa League and the Europa League berth reserved for the DFB-Pokal winners is transferred to the highest finisher below the Champions League qualification places.
<ul><li>Prior to 2015–16, the team that benefited from that rule did not necessarily have to be a Bundesliga member. For example, although <a href="/wiki/2._Bundesliga" title="2. Bundesliga">2. Bundesliga</a> sides <a href="/wiki/Alemannia_Aachen" title="Alemannia Aachen">Alemannia Aachen</a> lost to <a href="/wiki/SV_Werder_Bremen" title="SV Werder Bremen">Werder Bremen</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2003%E2%80%9304_DFB-Pokal#Final" title="2003–04 DFB-Pokal">2004 DFB-Pokal Final</a>, Alemannia secured an entry in the <a href="/wiki/2004%E2%80%9305_UEFA_Cup" title="2004–05 UEFA Cup">2004–05 UEFA Cup</a>, because Werder qualified for the Champions League as First Bundesliga champions.</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>The number of German clubs which may participate in UEFA competitions is determined by <a href="/wiki/UEFA_coefficients" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA coefficients">UEFA coefficients</a>, which take into account the results of a particular nation's clubs in UEFA competitions over the preceding five years.
</p>
<dl><dt>History of European qualification</dt></dl>
<ul><li>European Cup/Champions League:
<ul><li>Up to and including 1996–97: German champion only.</li>
<li>1997–99: Top two teams; champions automatically into group phase, runners-up entered the qualifying round.</li>
<li>1999–2008: Top two teams automatically into first group phase (only one group phase starting in 2003–04). Depending on the DFB's <a href="/wiki/UEFA_coefficients" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA coefficients">UEFA coefficients</a> standing, either one or two other clubs (most recently one) entered at the third qualifying round; winners at this level entered the group phase.</li>
<li>2008–11: Top two teams automatically into group phase. Third placed team had to play in the play-off round for the right to play in the group stage.</li></ul></li>
<li>UEFA Cup/Europa League:
<ul><li>From 1971–72 to 1998–99, UEFA member nations could send between one and four teams to the UEFA Cup. Germany was always entitled to send at least three teams to the competition and often as many as four. From 1978–79, the number of participants was determined by the DFB's UEFA coefficient standing, prior to this the method for deciding the number of participants is unknown. The best performing teams in the league other than the champion would qualify, although if one of these teams was also winner of the <a href="/wiki/DFB-Pokal" title="DFB-Pokal">DFB-Pokal</a> then they would enter the Cup Winners' Cup instead and their UEFA Cup place would be taken by the next highest-placed team in the league (5th or 6th place). Briefly in the mid-1970s the DFB decided to allocate the last UEFA Cup place to the DFB-Pokal runner-up instead of a third or fourth team qualified by performance in the league, meaning that at this point the DFB-Pokal qualified two teams for European competition (winners for the Cup Winners' Cup, runners-up for the UEFA Cup). This policy was unique amongst UEFA member associations and was dropped after only a few seasons. Starting with the 1999–2000 season and the abolition of the Cup Winners' Cup (which was then folded into the UEFA Cup), the DFB-Pokal winner now automatically qualified for the UEFA Cup alongside, depending on the DFB's UEFA coefficients standing, between one and three extra participants (if the DFB-Pokal winner also qualified for the Champions League, they were replaced by the DFB-Pokal runner-up; if they were also qualified for the Champions League, the UEFA Cup place went to the next best placed team in the league not otherwise qualified for European competition). Since 1999, the DFB has always been entitled to enter a minimum of three clubs in the UEFA Cup/Europa League, and at times as many as four (the maximum for any European federation). Teams that entered via UEFA's Fair Play mechanism, or those that entered through the now-defunct <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Intertoto_Cup" title="UEFA Intertoto Cup">Intertoto Cup</a>, did not count against the national quota. From <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Intertoto_Cup_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA Intertoto Cup 2006">2006</a> through the final Intertoto Cup in <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Intertoto_Cup_2008" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA Intertoto Cup 2008">2008</a>, only one First Bundesliga side was eligible to enter the Intertoto Cup and possibly earn a UEFA Cup berth. For the 2005–06 season, the DFB earned an extra UEFA Cup place via the Fair Play draw; this place went to <a href="/wiki/1._FSV_Mainz_05" title="1. FSV Mainz 05">Mainz 05</a> as the highest-ranked club in the Fair Play table of the First Bundesliga not already qualified for Europe.</li></ul></li>
<li>Cup Winners' Cup (abolished after 1999):
<ul><li>The winner of the DFB-Pokal entered the Cup Winners' Cup, unless that team was also league champion and therefore competing in the European Cup/Champions League, in which case their place in the Cup Winners' Cup was taken by the DFB-Pokal runner-up. Today, the DFB-Pokal winner (if not otherwise qualified for the Champions League) enters the UEFA Europa League.</li></ul></li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_model">Business model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Business model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the 2009–10 season the Bundesliga's turnover was €1.7bn, broken down into match-day revenue (€424m), sponsorship receipts (€573m) and broadcast income (€594m). That year it was the only European football league where clubs collectively made a profit. Bundesliga clubs paid less than 50% of revenue in players wages, the lowest percentage out of the European leagues. The Bundesliga has the lowest ticket prices and the highest average attendance among Europe's five major leagues.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bundesliga clubs tend to form close associations with local firms, several of which have since grown into big global companies; in a comparison of leading Bundesliga and Premiership clubs, Bayern Munich received 55% of its revenue from company sponsorship deals, while Manchester United got 37%.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Evans_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg1_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg1-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg2_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bundesliga clubs are required to be majority-owned by German club members (known as the <a href="/wiki/50%2B1_rule" title="50+1 rule">50+1 rule</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/50%2B1-Regel" class="extiw" title="de:50+1-Regel">de</a>&#93;</span> to discourage control by a single entity) and operate under tight restrictions on the use of debt for acquisitions (a team only receives an operating licence if it has solid financials), as a result 11 of the 18 clubs were profitable after the 2008–09 season. By contrast, in the other major European leagues numerous high-profile teams have come under ownership of foreign billionaires and a significant number of clubs have high levels of debt.<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg1_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg1-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg2_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Exceptions to the 50+1 rule allow <a href="/wiki/Bayer_04_Leverkusen" title="Bayer 04 Leverkusen">Bayer Leverkusen</a>, <a href="/wiki/TSG_1899_Hoffenheim" title="TSG 1899 Hoffenheim">TSG 1899 Hoffenheim</a>, and <a href="/wiki/VfL_Wolfsburg" title="VfL Wolfsburg">VfL Wolfsburg</a> to be owned by corporations or individual investors. In the cases of Bayer Leverkusen and Wolfsburg, the clubs were founded by major corporations (respectively <a href="/wiki/Bayer_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="Bayer AG">Bayer AG</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a>) as sports clubs for their employees, while Hoffenheim has long received its primary support from <a href="/wiki/SAP_SE" class="mw-redirect" title="SAP SE">SAP</a> co-founder <a href="/wiki/Dietmar_Hopp" title="Dietmar Hopp">Dietmar Hopp</a>, who played in the club's youth system.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After 2000 the <a href="/wiki/German_Football_Association" title="German Football Association">German Football Association</a> and the Bundesliga required every club to run a <a href="/wiki/Youth_academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Youth academy">youth academy</a> with the aim of developing local talent for the club and the national team. As of 2010 the Bundesliga and second Bundesliga spend €75m a year on these youth academies, which train five thousand players aged 12–18. This increased the percentage of under-23-year-olds in the Bundesliga from 6% in 2000 to 15% in 2010. This in turn allows more money to be spent on the smaller number of players that are bought.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg1_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg1-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg2_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2000s, the Bundesliga was regarded as competitive, as five teams won the league title. This contrasted with the English <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Premier League</a>, then dominated by a "Big Four" (Manchester United, Chelsea, Liverpool, and Arsenal), as well as France's <a href="/wiki/Ligue_1" title="Ligue 1">Ligue 1</a>, won seven consecutive years by <a href="/wiki/Olympique_Lyonnais" title="Olympique Lyonnais">Lyon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In the second decade, however, a resurgent <a href="/wiki/Bayern_Munich" class="mw-redirect" title="Bayern Munich">Bayern Munich</a> has won each year from 2013 to 2021 onward.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_regulations">Financial regulations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Financial regulations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For a number of years, the clubs in the Bundesliga have been subject to regulations not unlike the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Financial_Fair_Play_Regulations" title="UEFA Financial Fair Play Regulations">UEFA Financial Fair Play Regulations</a> agreed upon in September 2009.
</p><p>At the end of each season, clubs in the Bundesliga must apply to the <a href="/wiki/German_Football_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="German Football Federation">German Football Federation</a> (DFB) for a licence to participate again the following year; only when the DFB, who have access to all transfer documents and accounts, are satisfied that there is no threat of insolvency do they give approval.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The DFB have a system of fines and points deductions for clubs who flout rules and those who go into the red can only buy a player after selling one for at least the same amount. In addition, no individual is allowed to own more than 49 per cent of any Bundesliga club, the only exceptions being <a href="/wiki/VfL_Wolfsburg" title="VfL Wolfsburg">VfL Wolfsburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bayer_Leverkusen" class="mw-redirect" title="Bayer Leverkusen">Bayer Leverkusen</a> and current <a href="/wiki/3._Liga" title="3. Liga">3. Liga</a> member <a href="/wiki/FC_Carl_Zeiss_Jena" title="FC Carl Zeiss Jena">FC Carl Zeiss Jena</a> should they ever be promoted to the Bundesliga as they were each founded as factory teams.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MJL_Waldstadion_2006_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/MJL_Waldstadion_2006_3.jpg/280px-MJL_Waldstadion_2006_3.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/MJL_Waldstadion_2006_3.jpg/420px-MJL_Waldstadion_2006_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/MJL_Waldstadion_2006_3.jpg/560px-MJL_Waldstadion_2006_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2556" data-file-height="932" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MJL_Waldstadion_2006_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Commerzbank_Arena" class="mw-redirect" title="Commerzbank Arena">Commerzbank Arena</a>, is the home ground of <a href="/wiki/Eintracht_Frankfurt" title="Eintracht Frankfurt">Eintracht Frankfurt</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Despite the good economic governance, there have still been some instances of clubs getting into difficulties. In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Borussia_Dortmund" title="Borussia Dortmund">Borussia Dortmund</a> reported a debt of €118.8&#160;million (£83&#160;million).<sup id="cite_ref-Daily_Telegraph_17_November_2004_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Telegraph_17_November_2004-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Having won the Champions League in 1997 and a number of Bundesliga titles, Dortmund had gambled on maintaining their success with an expensive group of largely foreign players but failed, narrowly escaping liquidation in 2006. In subsequent years, the club went through extensive restructuring to return to financial health, largely with young home-grown players. In 2004 <a href="/wiki/Hertha_BSC" title="Hertha BSC">Hertha BSC</a> reported debts of £24.7&#160;million and were able to continue in the Bundesliga only after proving they had long term credit with their bank.<sup id="cite_ref-Daily_Telegraph_17_November_2004_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Telegraph_17_November_2004-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The leading German club <a href="/wiki/FC_Bayern_Munich" title="FC Bayern Munich">Bayern Munich</a> made a net profit of just €2.5&#160;million in 2008–09 season (group accounts),<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/FC_Schalke_04" title="FC Schalke 04">Schalke 04</a> made a net loss of €30.4&#160;million in 2009 financial year.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Borussia_Dortmund" title="Borussia Dortmund">Borussia Dortmund</a> GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA, made a net loss of just €2.9&#160;million in 2008–09 season.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attendances">Attendances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Attendances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bundesliga_attendance" title="Bundesliga attendance">Bundesliga attendance</a></div>
<p>Based on its per-game average, the Bundesliga is the best-attended association football league in the world; out of all sports, its average of 45,116 fans per game during the 2011–12 season was the second highest of any <a href="/wiki/Professional_sports" title="Professional sports">professional sports</a> league worldwide, behind only the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a> of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-sportbusiness1_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sportbusiness1-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Bundesliga club <a href="/wiki/Borussia_Dortmund" title="Borussia Dortmund">Borussia Dortmund</a> has the highest average attendance of any football club in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Out of Europe's five major football leagues (<a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Premier League</a>, <a href="/wiki/La_Liga" title="La Liga">La Liga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ligue_1" title="Ligue 1">Ligue 1</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Serie_A" title="Serie A">Serie A</a> ), the Bundesliga has the lowest ticket prices and the highest average attendance. Many club stadia have large terraced areas for <a href="/wiki/Safe_standing" title="Safe standing">standing fans</a> (by comparison, stadia in the English <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Premier League</a> are all-seaters due to the <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Report" title="Taylor Report">Taylor Report</a>). Teams limit the number of <a href="/wiki/Season_ticket" title="Season ticket">season tickets</a> to ensure everyone has a chance to see the games live, and the away club has the right to 10% of the available capacity. Match tickets often double as free rail passes which encourages supporters to travel and celebrate in a relaxed atmosphere. According to Bundesliga chief executive <a href="/wiki/Christian_Seifert" title="Christian Seifert">Christian Seifert</a>, tickets are inexpensive (especially for <a href="/wiki/Safe_standing" title="Safe standing">standing room</a>) as "It is not in the clubs' culture so much [to raise prices]. They are very fan orientated".<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg1_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg1-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg2_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Uli_Hoene%C3%9F" title="Uli Hoeneß">Uli Hoeneß</a>, president of Bayern Munich, was quoted as saying "We do not think the fans are like cows to be milked. Football has got to be for everybody."<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Borussia_Dortmund_Hannover_96.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Borussia_Dortmund_Hannover_96.jpg/220px-Borussia_Dortmund_Hannover_96.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Borussia_Dortmund_Hannover_96.jpg/330px-Borussia_Dortmund_Hannover_96.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Borussia_Dortmund_Hannover_96.jpg/440px-Borussia_Dortmund_Hannover_96.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Borussia_Dortmund_Hannover_96.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Bundesliga has the highest average attendance of any football league in the world. <a href="/wiki/Borussia_Dortmund" title="Borussia Dortmund">Borussia Dortmund</a> has the highest average attendance at <a href="/wiki/Westfalenstadion" title="Westfalenstadion">Signal Iduna Park</a> of any football club in the world.</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_coverage">Media coverage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Media coverage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic">Domestic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Domestic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Bundesliga TV, radio, internet, and mobile broadcast rights are distributed by DFL Sports Enterprises, a subsidiary of the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Fu%C3%9Fball_Liga" title="Deutsche Fußball Liga">Deutsche Fußball Liga</a>. The Bundesliga broadcast rights are sold along with the broadcast rights to the relegation playoffs, <a href="/wiki/2._Bundesliga" title="2. Bundesliga">2. Bundesliga</a> and <a href="/wiki/DFL-Supercup" title="DFL-Supercup">DFL-Supercup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 2017 to 2018 to 2018–19, Bundesliga matches were broadcast on TV in Germany on <a href="/wiki/Sky_Deutschland" title="Sky Deutschland">Sky Deutschland</a> and Eurosport. Prior to the 2019–20 season, Eurosport sublicensed its broadcast rights to sports streaming service <a href="/wiki/DAZN" title="DAZN">DAZN</a>, which will broadcast games previously allocated to Eurosport until the conclusion of the 2020–21 season.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Three Friday night matches – the openers of the first and second halves of the season, and on the final matchday before the winter break – are broadcast to all Germans on <a href="/wiki/ZDF" title="ZDF">ZDF</a>.
</p><p>Starting with the 2018–19 season, Sky began arranging simulcasts of high-profile Saturday games on free TV to promote its coverage of the league. The April 2019 <a href="/wiki/Revierderby" title="Revierderby">Revierderby</a> was broadcast on <a href="/wiki/Das_Erste" title="Das Erste">Das Erste</a>, and two additional games during the 2019–20 season were broadcast on ZDF.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<p>Radio coverage includes the national <i>Konferenz</i> (whip-around coverage) on the stations of <a href="/wiki/ARD_(broadcaster)" title="ARD (broadcaster)">ARD</a> and full match coverage on local radio stations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global">Global</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Global">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SC_Freiburg_vs_FSVMainz_17_ao%C3%BBt_2013_60.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/SC_Freiburg_vs_FSVMainz_17_ao%C3%BBt_2013_60.jpg/220px-SC_Freiburg_vs_FSVMainz_17_ao%C3%BBt_2013_60.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/SC_Freiburg_vs_FSVMainz_17_ao%C3%BBt_2013_60.jpg/330px-SC_Freiburg_vs_FSVMainz_17_ao%C3%BBt_2013_60.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/SC_Freiburg_vs_FSVMainz_17_ao%C3%BBt_2013_60.jpg/440px-SC_Freiburg_vs_FSVMainz_17_ao%C3%BBt_2013_60.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4918" data-file-height="3279" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SC_Freiburg_vs_FSVMainz_17_ao%C3%BBt_2013_60.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Bundesliga is broadcast on TV in over 200 countries</div></div></div>
<p>The Bundesliga is broadcast on TV in over 200 countries. <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> has held rights in the United States since the beginning of the 2020–21 season. 4 matches per season are reserved for linear television with the rest appearing on <a href="/wiki/ESPN%2B" title="ESPN+">ESPN+</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In Canada, broadcast rights were sub-licensed to <a href="/wiki/Sportsnet" title="Sportsnet">Sportsnet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sportsnet_World" title="Sportsnet World">Sportsnet World</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sportsnet-bundesliga_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sportsnet-bundesliga-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom and in Ireland, the Bundesliga is broadcast live on <a href="/wiki/Sky_Sports" title="Sky Sports">Sky Sports</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In Spain, the Bundesliga is broadcast live on <a href="/wiki/Movistar%2B" class="mw-redirect" title="Movistar+">Movistar+</a>.
</p><p>In 2015, digital TV operator <a href="/wiki/StarTimes" title="StarTimes">StarTimes</a> acquired exclusive television rights for Sub-Saharan Africa for five years starting from 2015 to 2016 season.<sup id="cite_ref-sportsindustry-bundesliga_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sportsindustry-bundesliga-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Champions">Champions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Champions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_German_football_champions" title="List of German football champions">List of German football champions</a></div>
<p>In total, 43 clubs have won the <i>German championship</i>, including titles won before the Bundesliga's inception and those in the <a href="/wiki/DDR-Oberliga" title="DDR-Oberliga">East German Oberliga</a>. The record champions are <a href="/wiki/FC_Bayern_Munich" title="FC Bayern Munich">Bayern Munich</a> with 31 titles,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> ahead of <a href="/wiki/Berliner_FC_Dynamo" title="Berliner FC Dynamo">BFC Dynamo</a> with 10 (all in the DDR-Oberliga) and <a href="/wiki/1._FC_N%C3%BCrnberg" title="1. FC Nürnberg">1. FC Nürnberg</a> with 9.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours">Honours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Honours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Kahn_of_Munich,_September_2,_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Oliver_Kahn_of_Munich%2C_September_2%2C_2008.jpg/220px-Oliver_Kahn_of_Munich%2C_September_2%2C_2008.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Oliver_Kahn_of_Munich%2C_September_2%2C_2008.jpg/330px-Oliver_Kahn_of_Munich%2C_September_2%2C_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Oliver_Kahn_of_Munich%2C_September_2%2C_2008.jpg/440px-Oliver_Kahn_of_Munich%2C_September_2%2C_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Kahn_of_Munich,_September_2,_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Kahn" title="Oliver Kahn">Oliver Kahn</a> won eight Bundesliga championships</div></div></div>
<p>In 2004, the honour of "Verdiente Meistervereine" (roughly "distinguished champion clubs") was introduced, following a custom first practised in Italy<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> to recognise sides that have won three or more championships since 1963 by the display of <a href="/wiki/Star_(football_badge)" class="mw-redirect" title="Star (football badge)">gold stars on their team badges</a> and jerseys. Each country's usage is unique, with the following rules applying in Germany:<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>3 Bundesliga titles: 1 star</li>
<li>5 Bundesliga titles: 2 stars</li>
<li>10 Bundesliga titles: 3 stars</li>
<li>20 Bundesliga titles: 4 stars</li>
<li>30 Bundesliga titles: 5 stars</li></ul>
<p>The former East German side <a href="/wiki/BFC_Dynamo" class="mw-redirect" title="BFC Dynamo">BFC Dynamo</a> laid claim to the three stars of a 10-time champion. The club asked for equal rights and petitioned the DFL and  the DFB to have their <a href="/wiki/DDR-Oberliga" title="DDR-Oberliga">DDR-Oberliga</a> titles recognised. BFC Dynamo received support from SG Dynamo Dresden and 1. FC Magdeburg in its attempts to achieve recognition for East German titles.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The DFL eventually answered that it was not the responsible body and pointed to the DFB, but the DFB remained silent for long time. BFC Dynamo eventually took matters into their own hands and emblazoned its jerseys with three stars, while a decision was still pending.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> This caused some debate because the club had been the favourite club of <a href="/wiki/Erich_Mielke" title="Erich Mielke">Erich Mielke</a> during the East German era. There were rumours that the ten titles won by the club were also due to alleged manipulation of the game by Erich Mielke, while there is no proof that referees stood under direct instructions from the <a href="/wiki/Stasi" title="Stasi">Stasi</a> and no document has ever been found in the archives that gave the Stasi a mandate to bribe referees.<sup id="cite_ref-StarQuality_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarQuality-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Critics in the DFB environment pointed to politically influenced championships in East Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-KriegSterne_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KriegSterne-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> BFC Dynamo had been supported by the Stasi and had been advantaged.<sup id="cite_ref-KriegSterne_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KriegSterne-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The club had enjoyed privileged access to talents and access to a permanent training camp at Uckley in <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigs_Wusterhausen" title="Königs Wusterhausen">Königs Wusterhausen</a>. However, also other clubs in East Germany had enjoyed similar advantages, which put the DFB in a difficult situation.<sup id="cite_ref-KriegSterne_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KriegSterne-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, former East German referee and <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democratic_Union_of_Germany" title="Christian Democratic Union of Germany">CDU</a> parliamentarian <a href="/wiki/Bernd_Heynemann" title="Bernd Heynemann">Bernd Heynemann</a> spoke out for recognition of all East German titles.<sup id="cite_ref-StarQuality_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarQuality-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The issue of recognition for titles outside the Bundesliga also affected pre-Bundesliga champions, such as <a href="/wiki/Hertha_BSC" title="Hertha BSC">Hertha BSC</a>. The DFB finally decided in November 2005 to allow <a href="/wiki/German_football_champions" class="mw-redirect" title="German football champions">all former champions</a> to display a single star inscribed with the number of titles, including all German men's titles since 1903, <a href="/wiki/Frauen-Bundesliga" title="Frauen-Bundesliga">women's titles</a> since 1974 and East German titles.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The DFB format only applies to teams playing below the Bundesliga (below the top two divisions), since the DFL conventions apply in the Bundesliga. <a href="/wiki/SpVgg_Greuther_F%C3%BCrth" title="SpVgg Greuther Fürth">Greuther Fürth</a> unofficially display three (silver) stars for pre-war titles in spite of being in the Bundesliga. These stars are a permanent part of their crest. However, Fürth has to leave the stars out of their jersey.
</p><p>Since June 2010, the following clubs have been officially allowed to wear stars while playing in the Bundesliga. The number in parentheses is for Bundesliga titles won.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:Star_full.svg" class="image"><img alt="Star full.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/20px-Star_full.svg.png" decoding="async" <p>In addition, a system of one star designation was adopted for use. This system is intended to take into account not only Bundesliga titles but also other (now defunct) national championships. As of July 2014, the following clubs are allowed to wear one star while playing outside the Bundesliga. The number in parentheses is for total league championships won over the course of German football history, and would be displayed within the star. Some teams listed here had different names while winning their respective championships, these names are also noted in parentheses.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Logo_history">Logo history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Logo history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For the first time in 1996, the Bundesliga was given its own logo to distinguish itself. Six years later, the logo was revamped into a portrait orientation, which was used until 2010. A new logo was announced for the <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9311_Bundesliga" title="2010–11 Bundesliga">2010–11 season</a> in order to modernise the brand logo for all media platforms.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> To celebrate the 50th anniversary of the Bundesliga, a special logo was developed for the <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_Bundesliga" title="2012–13 Bundesliga">2012–13 season</a>, featuring a "50" and "1963–2013".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Following the season, the 2010 logo was restored. In December 2016, it was announced that a new logo would be used for the 2017–18 season, modified slightly for digitisation requirements, featuring a matte look.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_and_criticism">Influence and criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bundesliga&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Influence and criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Dutch football schools, which existed and developed the Netherlands into one of Europe and the world's major football forces, have been strongly influenced and galvanised with German football philosophy, in particular by experiences of Dutch players and managers in Bundesliga.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Former England international <a href="/wiki/Owen_Hargreaves" title="Owen Hargreaves">Owen Hargreaves</a> hailed the Bundesliga alongside <a href="/wiki/Pep_Guardiola" title="Pep Guardiola">Pep Guardiola</a> for its positive impact on nurturing young talents, noting that the Bundesliga is the best league in the world to promote young footballers.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Many young English talents have sought refuge in Germany in order to regain fitness and football skills.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Outside Europe, the <a href="/wiki/J.League" title="J.League">J.League</a> of Japan, which was founded in 1992, was strongly influenced by the philosophy of the Bundesliga. Since then, the J.League has managed to establish itself as one of the best football leagues in Asia, in which it shares a beneficial relationship with its German counterpart.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Bundesliga has earned praise for its reputation on good financial management and the physical fitness of players.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Bundesliga outperformed the English <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Premier League</a> in 2017 in online influence in China, having been accredited for its open embrace of live-streaming and fast-forward visions.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Bundesliga has at times been criticised for a perceived lack of competitiveness due to the continued dominance of FC Bayern Munich. The club has won a record 31 titles in the modern Bundesliga era since 1963; a greater level of success than their rivals by a considerable margin. Indeed, the Bavarian club has won every consecutive title since the 2011–12 season.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Former Germany international <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Effenberg" title="Stefan Effenberg">Stefan Effenberg</a> has suggested that the league be restructured in order to end Bayern's dominance.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
